state of madness

you should never proof anything before you believe in it

  • there is actually no real world, and mathematics might be a more realistic world
    • doing mathematics is like exploring a very different universe
    • there is nothing. forget yourself, forget the world
    • sometimes you feel upset because sometimes you cannot understand some theorem or materials, but think again why you read math the first time, it is because depressed about the humanlity. now you are getting “human”. just fight it. like a Brute force algorithm
    • the aim is aimless. it is just inmense yourself in the mathematical world
  • active
    • always thinking, internalize things, always rigid, make real progress, pick things on the way
    • mathematical truth can be discovered just as experimental science, we try examples see the result, make a conjuncture, and proof it
    • i want to solve problems http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_unsolved_problems_in_mathematics
    • you should always proof the whole book out and do all exercises
    • as we know axiomatic method is not enough, so do study examples, and often example produce theory
    • constructive
  • reactive
    • learn things when needed, this is the most efficient way, you can immediately see it used in practice
    • when use old results, dig deeper in the chain. because it is hopeless to check the state of old material
  • ensure you understand
    • mental image of the mathematical world, intuition
      • It’s like a new toaster that comes with a 16-page manual. If you already understand toasters and if the toaster looks like previous toasters you’ve encountered, you might just plug it in and see if it works, rather than first reading all the details in the manual.
      • get intuition, and get rigid and formal
      • about mathematical experience. mathematics is elegant, if you do not think something natural, you do not understand it. remember when you understand group action
      • but today after reading Atiyah’s article and what Huajie said, i think i should learn more geometry
      • i still feel abstract… i study rings, modules, but can hardly find enough examples of ring or module… i know that’s a tensor product, but can hardly know what it looks like, i cannot make mathematics into me… feeling sad. as CY said, after studying a subject, you should be able to write down all things you have meet
      • every thing should be evident
    • understand the problem
      • they said the Grothendieck do not solve problems, he found out a comfort space to the problem to stay at, but even working out the details is hard for me. is he had some intuition for abstraction???
      • give the conjectures their full meaning
      • fully see what you have get. use the tools at hand. it might do not directly leads you to the solution, but anyway use it!
      • see the logic of the problem. assume the solution, and see why it works, or what’s wrong with it
      • the reason that we construct so many constructions is that we concrete our proof process by making them actually shown before your eyes, by constructions
  • symbolic, not understanding. sometimes you just do not understand it now
    • how to proof? it is sufficient to make sure you can write all rigid sentences. you can omitted the writing, but you should always make sure you can freely think rigidly. only idea is not enough
  • npo
    • the static and dynamic structure of the mathematical world is not the same, so you have multiple views
      • it is always helpful to view the picture of mathematics as static, there are no such thing \(x+y\), it is just an element. for example, finite addition is always well defined, because we can write finitely. but it is not infinite addition. we should rely on a map from \({\mathbb{N}}\) to the group, then show how the addtion is performed and ended
      • i feel that every time i use Zorn’s lemma, i feel a little more depressed, because i am out of the world where mathematical entities lives
  • know where you are
    • i think the best state of doing mathematics is always have the big picture in background, but in the same time, always solving details and problems, thus making real progress
    • i feel my learning mathematics is just like a “guided tour”, actually i do not know why guided like this. thinking how G create mathematics single handed. i think i am just lame
  • if you cannot do these, DO NOT WASTE YOUR TIME!!
    • when you found yourself rereading a page over and over again, you are not ready for this book, example, May
    • do not read proofs that you are not going to understand!!!

about mathematical foundation

just do not care about it now, the foundation is some kind of syntax, the encode, but what’s really about mathematics, mathematics is about understanding, is a view of the Patonic mathematical world

informal notes about mathematical logic and foundation. 2014-04

  • why there are independent theorems? it can be true, can be not true, but has no impact on us. in other words, logic is just a tool. basically we
  • considering mathematics questions and answers as a a list of strings, then we are in 4 kind of situation. this is just a asymmetric, and Godel’s result states that there is always the situation.
    1. we proof/ disproof it
    2. we proofed that we cannot proof
    3. we do not know if we can proof it or not i do not study mathematical logic, i just pick a certain foundation i like, but some issues must be addressed. these are some ideas that i partially understand, to comfort me
  • “Mathematicians do not study objects, but the relations between objects; to them it is a matter of indifference if these objects are replaced by others, provided that the relations do not change. Matter does not engage their attention, they are interested by form alone.”
  • how to proof CH do not entail ZFC? we construct a model of ZFC, such that nCH is true. and how to proof nCH do not entail ZFC? we construct a model of ZFC such that CH is true, then certainly nCH do not entail from ZFC. but how to proof. because we are constructing model in ZFC, so if ZFC is consistent, then we know that we can logically deduce to CH or nCH. a axiomatic system is consistent if it has a model, so we know that both ZFC + CH and ZFC + nCH is consistent (if ZFC is)
  • so most mathematics we study is just a very tiny fraction of the mathematical universe (ZFC). but when we ask basic questions about the big picture (ZFC). it often happens that we cannot know it at all. the thing is think the model – ZFC, \({\mathbb{Z}}\) – group. as long as we approach the bottom of our logic. we found a similar situation as we are in group: is group commutative? we found that kind of question unanswerable, just as asking is CH true for all models of sets
  • but… only if ZFC is consistent
    • a thought experiment. as all model of ZFC is constructed inside ZFC, can we add a axiom: if a model of itself has a proposition independent…. is this self-reference impossible?
  • is our universe un-understandable? actually if i have reached a point such that no-contradiction happens, i will just be happy, because scientific falsehood is ensured by experiment, but scientific truth is not… if there is a “incompleteness theorem” for the universe. we reached here or not will never matter to us, because we will always in a state of waiting for more experiment. though the situation in mathematics is not the same. so i think i have a happy foundation for things i care, but as a mathematical system can never proof it’s consistency, we are actually in the best situation possible no inconsistency have found now in ZFC. i think i will only consider stop learning mathematics when we have proofed something like “no axiomatic system stronger or equal to Peano arithmetic is consistent”
  • category foundation
    • category in set theory
      • the metacategory view, if we define them axiomatic, then we are having a method of mathematical language, but they are not objects inside mathematics, for example, we can view all sets as a metacategory
      • the universe view, we can make a background universe, and works inside it
      • i want to avoid these issues, i do not like them
    • in type theory
      • it is not good, defined pre-category and category. but at least we do not have to think about small and big category

more informal notes. 2014-05

  • now i actually do not think is there any foundational crisis in mathematics back in time, it is just a Russell paradox, and the solution of ZFC is ugly

idea

  • i want to take homotopy type theory as the foundation of mathematics, it is a good idea, but no one knows exactly how to do it now. but nonetheless i will present ideas in it. it should be understand that, all mathematics we do now will have a good old foundation in ZFC set theory, as long as we ignore the issues with categories. so basically when we finished type theory, set theory, and category theory, we should have our formalism in mind
  • but we do not do constructive math, because i do not like it. though constructive view is good
  • the notes here is only to capture an idea of type theory, and conform myself about the ugliness of ZFC. it is not rigor, but nonetheless i will use it now!
  • by a foundation, it is need to accept the system intuitively. in the other side, we have a axiomic set theory, and we have model in it, the path is first have a formal model of it, then we have the axiomic theory, and then we might play with inner model. the first stage is a must, because currently it boost your understanding of type theory

type theory

they are just syntax now. the real understanding should be the semantics

  • type theory
    • context \(x_1: A_1, x_2: A_2,\dots, x_n:A_n\)
      • \({\text{ctx}}\)-EMP \(\frac{}{\cdot {\text{ctx}}}\)
      • \({\text{ctx}}\)-EXT \(\frac{\Gamma\vdash A_n: {\mathcal{U}}_i}{(\Gamma, A_n: {\mathcal{U}}_i){\text{ctx}}}\)
    • judgment
      • \(\Gamma {\text{ctx}}\)
      • \(\Gamma\vdash a: A\)
      • \(\Gamma\vdash a\equiv a': A\)
    • structural rule
    • inference rule this is not definition, actually all rules for types is independently defined, but share a common structure
      • type rule
      • introduction rule
      • elimination rule
      • computation rule
    • derivation
    • type are higher groupoids
      • universe
        • \({\mathcal{U}}\)-INTRO \(\frac{\Gamma {\text{ctx}}}{\Gamma\vdash {\mathcal{U}}_i: {\mathcal{U}}_{i+1}}\)
        • \({\mathcal{U}}\)-CUMUL \(\frac{\Gamma\vdash A: {\mathcal{U}}_i}{\Gamma\vdash A: U_{i+1}}\)
        • universe polymorphism we use this trick to do justify why we are not using explicit universe level when doing informally. or how weak we are
      • function. actually they are functors
        • \(\Pi\)-FORM \(\frac{\Gamma\vdash A:{\mathcal{U}}_i\quad \Gamma, x: A\vdash B:{\mathcal{U}}_i}{\Gamma\vdash \Pi_{x:A}B:{\mathcal{U}}_i}\)
        • \(\Pi\)-INTRO \(\frac{\Gamma, x:A\vdash b:B}{\Gamma\vdash \lambda (x: A). b: \Pi_{x: A}B}\)
        • \(\Pi\)-ELIM \(\frac{\Gamma\vdash f: \Pi_{x:A}B\quad \Gamma\vdash a: A}{ \Gamma\vdash f(a): B[a/x]}\)
        • \(\Pi\)-COMP \(\frac{\Gamma, x:A\vdash b:B\quad \Gamma\vdash a:A}{\Gamma\vdash (\lambda(x:A).b)(a)\equiv b[a/x]: B[a/x]}\)
        • \(\Pi\)-UNIQ
        • you can think there are bunch of primitives, especially induction rules will confirm certain function exists, but actually the semantics is defined inside the theory
        • apply \({\text{ap}}: (f: A\to B) \to (x=_A y)\to (f(x) =_B f(y))\) exists, and \({\text{ap}}(f, {\text{refl}}_x)\equiv {\text{refl}}_{f(x)}\) \({\diamond}\) by path induction. so function is actually functors, required by \(J\). think like this, all primitives for inductive type will require you to respect equality, and when defining functions, we actually is always invoking a bunch of primitives, so…
          • \({\text{ap}}_f(p\cdot q) = {\text{ap}}_f(p)\cdot {\text{ap}}_f(q)\), …
        • transport type family \(P\) over \(A\) and \(p: x =_A y\) there is \(p_*: P(x)\to P(y)\)
          • \({\S}\) \({\text{isSet}}\)
          • \({\text{lift}}(u, p): (x, u) = (y, p_*(u))\) in type total space \(\sum_{x:A}P(x)\). the coproduct type is also non-trivial???. type section. type fiberwise
          • \({\text{apd}}_f: p_*(f(x))=_{P(y)} f(y)\)

        • type homotopy \((f \sim g)\) \(\prod_{x: A} f(x) = g(x)\) they are natural transforms

        • type quasi-inverse \({\text{qinv}}(f)\). \(g: B\to A\), \(\alpha: f\circ g \sim {\text{id}}_B\) and \(\beta\)
          • \((A\simeq B) = \sum_{f: A\to B} {\text{isequiv}}(f)\) ex
      • identity type the good properties of identity type is in the \(J\) rule, it is essentially if they are used in same way, then they are the same~
        • \(=\)-INTRO \({\text{refl}}\) the basic intro rule for a identity type, it is like identity morphism in category, and it really is:
          • \(p = p\cdot {\text{refl}}= {\text{refl}}\cdot p\) \({\diamond}\) path induction
        • \(=\)-ELIM
        • \(=\)-COMP \(J\)
          • op how to \(J\) compute with univalence axiom?
          • \((x = y)\to (y = z)\to (x = z)\) exists, and \({\text{refl}}_x\cdot{\text{refl}}_x \equiv {\text{refl}}_x\)
        • identity inverse for \(x = y\) we have a \(y = x\) \({\diamond}\) path induction
          • \(p^{-1}\cdot p = {\text{refl}}_y\), \(p\cdot p^{-1}={\text{refl}}_x\), \((p^{-1})^{-1} = p\) \({\diamond}\) path induction
        • type compose for \(p: x = y\), \(q: y = z\) we have \(p\cdot q: x = z\) \({\diamond}\) path induction, it is sufficient to assume \(x = y\) …
          • \(p\cdot (q\cdot r) = (p\cdot q)\cdot r\)
        • type loop space \(\Omega(A, a)\) is \(a=_A a\)

          • type Eckmann-Hilton the type compose of second loop space \(\Omega^2(A)\times \Omega^2(A)\to \Omega^2(A)\) is commutative \(\alpha\cdot \beta = \beta\cdot \alpha\)

            this picture is not for loop space!!!, imagine yourself. the type of \(\alpha: {\text{refl}}_a =_{a =_A a} {\text{refl}}_a\) and \(\beta: {\text{refl}}_a =_{a =_A a} {\text{refl}}_a\)

            \({\diamond}\) we have \(\alpha \star \beta: p\cdot r =q \cdot s\) by because \(\alpha: p = q\) we have \(p\cdot r = q\cdot r\) similarly we have \(q\cdot r = q\cdot s\), so composing we have \(\alpha\star\beta\). assuming they are all single element, we see… we have \(\alpha\cdot \beta = \alpha \star\beta = \alpha\star ' \beta = \beta\cdot \alpha\)

          • pointed type \((A, a)\)
      • higher inductive type

basic types

  • (-1)-type \({\text{isProp}}\doteq (P: {\mathcal{U}}) \to (x, y: P)\to (x = y)\)
    • \((P:{\mathcal{U}})\to {\text{isProp}}(P)\to {\text{isSet}}(P)\) ex
    • \((A:{\mathcal{U}})\to {\text{isProp}}({\text{isProp}}(P))\)
    • \((A:{\mathcal{U}})\to {\text{isProp}}({\text{isSet}}(A))\)
    • subtype \(P\) is type family of proposition then, \(\{x: A| P(x)\}\doteq \sum_{x:A} P(x)\), it is well defined for if \(x = y\) then \((x, p_x) = (y, p_y)\) \({\diamond}\) transposing
      • subuniverses \({\text{Set}}_{\mathcal{U}}\doteq \{s: U| {\text{isSet}}(s)\}\), \({\text{Prop}}_{\mathcal{U}}\), \({\text{Set}}_{{\mathcal{U}}_i}\to {\text{Set}}_{{\mathcal{U}}_{i+1}}\) is not equivalence
    • exclude in the middle \((P:{\text{Prop}}_{\mathcal{U}}, a: P)\to (a + \lnot a)\)
      • decidable if lem holds
    • propositional resizing mere proposition in lower universe inject to higher one is equivalence
    • propositional truncation for all type \(A\) we have \(\|A\|\), and if \(x:A\) then \(|a|:\|A\|\) and \(x, y: \|A\|\) then \(x = y\)
    • axiom of choice \((X: {\text{Set}}, Y: X\to {\text{Set}}_{\mathcal{U}})\to (\coprod _{x:X}\|Y(x)\|\to \|\coprod_{x:X} Y(x)\|)\)
  • contractible type \(\sum_{x: A}\coprod_{x:A}(a = x)\) is mere proposition
  • 0-type \({\text{isSet}}\doteq A: U_i\to (x, y: A)\to (p,q: x = y)\to (p = q)\)
  • natural number \({\mathbb{N}}\doteq (0: {\mathbb{N}}, s: {\mathbb{N}}\to {\mathbb{N}})\) is set \({\diamond}\) using encode-decode method

some universal definition

which i do not know where to put, they should be basic types, but i do not want to form them in types, just in sets now

  • law of composition we define here. because usually they are used in classical sense, it is just map \(S_1\times S_2\to S_3\)
    • associative \(abc = a(bc)\)
      • you can insert any parentheses
    • commutative \(ab = ba\) it seems then we must have \(S_1 = S_2 = S_3\)?
    • non-associative loc \({\S}\) \(-\) for integer
  • relation \(A\times A\to P\) where \(P\) is a proposition. we do not use boolean because a proposition is either inhabited or not, and contains more informations
    • equivalence relation
      • transitive \(a \sim b\land b\sim c{\Rightarrow}a\sim c\)
      • reflective \(a \sim a\)
      • symmetric \(a\sim b{\Rightarrow}b\sim a\)
      • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) partition
      • alternatively we have a quotient map. for any eqr, we can define the quotient map to map into the quotient class, and for any quotient map, we can define the eqr. categorically, it is far more convenient to use the quotient map

what if we are using a proof assistant?

i always feel messy, so i write informal notes now

package mathematics {

  package basic {


    package example of basic types {

      // we have universe polymorphism, so usually you should not worry about this, in this example, the two u is actually not the same one
      define universe: u = u

      // the simplest function definable now
      define function00 (a: u): u = a

      // you can leave out the return type when it can be inferred
      define function01 (a: u) = a

      // you can write parameters like this
      define function02 (a, b: u) (c: u) = b

      // or use lambda explicitly
      define function03 = lambda (a: u) => a

      // function apply
      define function04 (a, b: u) (f: a -> b) (x: a) = f a

      // implicit arguments
      define function05 {a, b: u} (f: a -> b) (x: a) = f a

      // compose
      define compose {a, b, c: u} (f: a -> b) (g: b -> c) = lambda (x: a) => g (f x)

      // uniqueness of function type, the two are definitional equal
      define uniqueOfFunctionType0 (a, b: u) (f: a -> b): (f = (lambda (x: a) => f a)) = refl f

      // basically you will be guiding the type checker how to write term
      define uniqueOfFunctionType1 (a, b, c, d: u) (f: a -> b) (g: b -> c) (h: c -> d): ((compose f (compose g h)) = (compose (compose f g) h)) = refl (compose (compose f g) h)



    }
  }
}

category

  • category or precategory?
    • it is unclear how we can do this inside type theory. should we always use category or precategory
    1. object (a type? set?)
      • small category this is a relative concept, some times the type is just a \(A:{\mathcal{U}}\), a small type, some time it is subuniverse of \({\mathcal{U}}_1: {\mathcal{U}}_2\), and we can think \({\mathcal{Cat}}_{{\mathcal{U}}_i}\) at any level
        • any small complete category is a preorder \({\diamond}\) assume there are \(r,s: x\to y\) then consider maps \(x\to \prod y\) the right product is of cardinal of the universe. then the maps has cardinal \(2^{|U|}\). so is not small
    2. morphism (a type? set?)
      • domain
      • codomain

      • for \(r\circ i={\text{id}}: X\to Y\to X\)

        • \(X\) or \(r\) is retraction of split monomorphism \(i\)

          • in arrow category we say \(f\) is retraction of \(g\), which means like

            • if \(g\) isomorphism, then \(f\) \({\diamond}\) the most easy proof is by moving the maps
            • \(r\) is retraction of \(i\circ r\)
        • \(i\) is selection of split epimorphism \(r\)
        • \({\Rightarrow}\) \(i\circ r\) is idempotent
      • endomorphism maps to self, so we have \(f^n\)
        • idempotent \(x^2 = x\)
      • isomorphism two morphism such that is inverse to each other
        • inverse unique \({\diamond}\) trivial
        • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) \(f\) having left inverse \(g\) having a left inverse
      • identity morphism \(\forall f: A\to B: {\text{id}}_B \circ f = f = f \circ {\text{id}}_A\)
        • unique \({\diamond}\) if one, must be it, if two, \(a= ab = b\)
        • \({\Rightarrow}\) idempotent
      • monomorphism \(=-\) then \(=\), cancel right
        • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) we have \({\text{id}}\) as pullback \({\diamond}\) dual
        • stable under base change \({\diamond}\) trivial, use the uniqueness of universal arrow
      • epimorphism \(-=\) then \(=\), cancel right

        • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) we have this pushout diagram \({\diamond}\) because it is epimorphism, so all cones actually is one arrow, so \({\text{id}}\) and \(Y\) is indeed a pushout. the reverse if trivial

        • stable under cobase change \({\diamond}\) dual
      • automorphism endomorphism and isomorphism
    3. associative composition law of morphism. so category is also algebraic
      • forgetting gives graph adjoint to free category
    4. exists identity morphism
    • shape of a category
      • category connected i think this thing is very interesting

category category

        • this is not sufficient, consider a functor maps to a constant and a idempotent

        • concrete category and concretizable \(({\mathcal{C}}, U:{\mathcal{C}}\to\mathcal{Set})\) and \(U\) is faithful functor

      • object functor
      • morphism natural transform for \(T,U:{\mathcal{D}}\to{\mathcal{C}}\), \(\tau: {\mathcal{D}}\to (T\downarrow U)\) such that \(P\circ \tau = Q\circ \tau = {\text{Id}}_{\mathcal{D}}\). natural in when restricted to a single object \(c\), for any \(f: c\to c'\) we have a single \(\tau_c: Tc\to Uc\)

      • arrow category \(({\text{Id}}\downarrow {\text{Id}})\). it is functor category \(\mathbf{2}\to {\mathcal{C}}\), so has a lot of good properties, for example, product and ccc. for \(f: A\to X\) and \(g: B\to Y\)

higher category

  • strict \(2\)-category every \({\text{hom}}(x, y)\) is again a category, and functors \(1\to {\text{hom}}(x, x)\) and \(c_{ABC}:{\text{hom}}(a, b)\times {\text{hom}}(b, c)\to {\text{hom}}(a, c)\), which satifies \(c_{ACD}\circ (c_{ABC}\times 1)=c_{ABD}\circ (1\times c_{BCD})\). inductively, strict \(n\)-category every \({\text{hom}}\) is a \((n-1)\)-category, the product is \(n\)-category product with \(n\)-functor fix me

three concept

universal arrow

  • universal arrow initial in comma category \((x\downarrow U:{\mathcal{D}}\to{\mathcal{C}})\), like correlated line and point, and morphism as morphism in \({\mathcal{D}}\)

    • it is universal arrow \({\Leftrightarrow}\) bijection in hom set \({\mathcal{D}}(r, d)\cong {\mathcal{C}}(c, Sd)\). then the bijection is natrual in \(d\). and any natural bijection is a universal arrow \({\diamond}\) iff is trivial.

      the natural is proofed by uniqueness of induced arrow. from natural isomorphism to universal arrow is by picking where \({\text{id}}\) is mapped

    • universal arrow is unique up to isomorphism, so is colimit and so is limit

Yoneda lemma

  • presheaf category \(F\in {\mathcal{Set}}^{{\mathcal{C}}^{\text{op}}}\)
    • representable presheaf isomorphism to contravariant hom-functor \({\mathcal{C}}(\_, B): {\mathcal{C}}^{\text{op}}\to {\mathcal{Set}}\)
      • maps colimits to limits. not the converse!!! \({\diamond}\) dual of representable functor
        • \({\mathcal{C}}(A, C)\times {\mathcal{C}}(B, C)\cong {\mathcal{C}}(A+B, C)\)
      • every presheaf is a colimit of representable presheafs \({\diamond}\) here fix me
    • elementary topos. complete and cocomplete fix me
    • contra Yoneda lemma \(y: {\mathcal{Set}}^{{\mathcal{C}}^{\text{op}}}({\mathcal{C}}(\_, c), F)\cong Fc\) natural in \(c\) and \(F\) \({\diamond}\) dual
    • Yoneda embedding \(y_{\mathcal{C}}: {\mathcal{C}}\to {\mathcal{Set}}^{{\mathcal{C}}^{\text{op}}}\) such that \(yC = {\text{hom}}_{\mathcal{C}}(\_, C)\) is full and faithful
      \({\diamond}\) we have \({\text{hom}}_{\mathcal{C}}(C, D) = yD(C)\cong {\text{hom}}(yC, yD)\) and we show the right side is \(yh\) for \(h: C\to D\). we have \(\vartheta_h(f: C'\to C) = yD(f)(h) = {\text{hom}}(f, D)(h)= h\circ f = (yh)_{C'}(f)\). so \(\vartheta_h = yh\)
      • Yoneda principle \(yA\cong yB{\Leftrightarrow}A\cong B\). the first one is natural transform!!! so remember to check natural in \(X\)!!!
        • actually, if you really think, the Yoneda principle is the most natrual thing
  • category \(K\in{\mathcal{Set}}^{\mathcal{C}}\)
    • representable functor isomorphism to hom-functor \({\mathcal{C}}(A, \_): {\mathcal{C}}\to{\mathcal{Set}}\)
      • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) there is a universal arrow \(u: *\to Kr\) for \(K: {\mathcal{C}}\to{\mathcal{Set}}\)
        \({\diamond}\) \(K\cong {\mathcal{C}}(r, \_){\Leftrightarrow}{\mathcal{Set}}(*, K \_ )\cong {\mathcal{C}}(r, \_){\Leftrightarrow}\) universal arrow
      • preserve all limits \({\diamond}\)
        1. \({\text{hom}}(C, *) \cong *\)
        2. \({\text{hom}}(C, \prod X_i) \cong \prod {\text{hom}}(C, X_i)\) \({\diamond}\) this is how we defined product morphism
        3. preserve equalizer

    • Yoneda lemma \(y: {\mathcal{Set}}^{\mathcal{C}}({\mathcal{C}}(c, \_), K)\cong Kc\) is a natural isomorphism of two functor \({\mathcal{Set}}^{\mathcal{C}}\times {\mathcal{C}}\to {\mathcal{Set}}\) \({\diamond}\)

      by first diagram, it is easily seen a bijection. the naturality is in the second, with induced maps pictured below. we need to proof for a natural transform \(\tau\) in the left-top conner, or \(G(h)[\theta_c(\tau_{{\text{id}}_c})] = \theta_d(\tau_h) = \theta_d[F(h)(\tau_{{\text{id}}_c})]\)

      • we set \(K={\mathcal{C}}(d, \_)\) we have all natural transform is of some \(f \in Kc = {\mathcal{C}}(d, c)\)

adjoint

  • adjoint from universal arrows for \(U:{\mathcal{D}}\to {\mathcal{C}}\), if every element of \({\mathcal{C}}\) has an universal arrow \(\eta_A: A\to UFA\), then

    1. a reverse functor \(F: {\mathcal{C}}\to {\mathcal{D}}\)

    2. \(\eta_A\) is natural in \(A\): \(\eta: {\text{Id}}\Rightarrow UF\)

    3. for \(Y\in {\mathcal{D}}\) we have in going universal arrow \(\epsilon_Y: FUY\to Y\) respective to \(F\)

    \({\diamond}\) to proof the universality, we notice that \((g^\flat)^\sharp = g\), we only need to show \(\epsilon_Y\circ Fg^\flat\) is the lift of \(g^\flat\), which is trivial from the universality of \(\eta\), i.e., you map back and verify that it is the lift
    1. \(\epsilon_Y\) is natural in \(Y\): \(\epsilon: FU\Rightarrow {\text{Id}}\)
    2. the reverse is also true
    3. we have \({\mathcal{D}}(FA, Y)\cong {\mathcal{C}}(A, UY)\) with \(f^\sharp \leftarrow f\) and \(g\rightarrow g^\flat\)
    4. we have for \(h: A'\to A\) and \(k: Y\to Y'\), \((f\circ h)^\sharp= f^\sharp\circ Fh\) and \((Uk\circ f)^\sharp = k\circ f^\sharp\). dually \((k\circ g)^\flat = Uk\circ g^\flat\) and \((g\circ Fh)^\flat = g^\flat \circ h\). so we have adjoint
    • adjoint determines universal arrow, and by one side of the universal arrow, we construct the other side, so adjoint determine each other
    • \(UX\) is naturally a retract of \(UFUX\)
      • \({\S}\) sometimes it is isomorphism, see cgwh

  • adjoint \(F\dashv U\), , \(U\) is right adjoint, \(F\) is left adjoint
    having a bijection \(\forall A\in {\mathcal{C}}\forall Y\in {\mathcal{D}}: \varphi_{A, Y}: {\mathcal{D}}(FA, Y)\cong {\mathcal{C}}(A, UY)\)
    \(\forall k: Y\to Y': \varphi(k\circ g) = Uk\circ \varphi(g)\)
    \(\forall h: A'\to A: \varphi(g\circ Fh)= \varphi(g)\circ h\)

    • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) \(\eta:{\text{Id}}_{\mathcal{C}}{\Rightarrow}UF\) and \(\epsilon: FU{\Rightarrow}{\text{Id}}_{\mathcal{D}}\) such that the diagram holds

      with definitions unit of adjoint \(\eta= \varphi({\text{id}}_F)\), counit of adjoint \(\epsilon =\varphi^{-1}({\text{id}}_U)\), and \(\eta_A: A\to UFA\) universal and \(\varphi(g) = Ug\circ \eta_A\) and \(\epsilon_Y:FUY\to Y\) universal and \(\varphi^{-1}(f) = \epsilon_Y\circ Ff\) \({\diamond}\)

      1. for any \(f: A\to UY\), we have \(f^\sharp = \varphi^{-1}(f)\) then \(f = Uf^\sharp \circ \varphi({\text{id}}_{FA})\), unique by \(f = Ug\circ \varphi^{-1}({\text{id}}_{FA})= \varphi(g)\). so every element has a universal arrow, so we have it

      2. \(U\epsilon_Y\circ \eta_{UY} = U\epsilon_Y\circ \varphi({\text{id}}_{FUY}) = \varphi(\epsilon_Y) ={\text{id}}_{UY}\)

      • notice that a lot of adjoint is actually produced by universal arrows, for example limits and dialogs, and exponentials and products
    • left adjoint is cocontinuous, right adjoint is continuous functor \({\diamond}\) for \(J: D\to {\mathcal{C}}\) \({\text{hom}}(X, U(\lim J))\cong {\text{hom}}(FX, \lim J)\cong \lim {\text{hom}}(FX, J) =\) \(\lim{\text{hom}}(X, U\circ J)\cong {\text{hom}}(X, \lim (U\circ J))\)

    • adjoint composition \(F'F\) and \(UU'\) \({\diamond}\) trivial, just show by the axiom

  • monand a endofunctor \(T:{\mathcal{C}}\to{\mathcal{C}}\), natural transform \(\eta: {\text{Id}}_{\mathcal{C}}\Rightarrow T\) and \(\mu: T^2 \Rightarrow T\) such that

    • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) a monoid object in \({\mathcal{C}}^{\mathcal{C}}\)
    • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) adjoint \({\diamond}\)
      1. adjoint to monad is trivial
      2. monad gives a adjoint, by constructing Eilenberg-Moore category \({\mathcal{C}}^T\)
        • it is not generally unique eg
    • idempotent momand \(\mu\) is natrual isomorphism fix me
    • monadic functor up to equivalence, is forgetful functor from em category. monadic adjoint

special adjoints

  • reflective subcategory a full subcategory \(i: {\mathcal{D}}{\hookrightarrow}{\mathcal{C}}\) having a left adjoint
    • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) counit is natural isomorphism fix me
    • \({\Rightarrow}\) \(i\) is monadic functor fix me
      • \({\Rightarrow}\) recoverd as em category of the idempotent monand
      • \({\Rightarrow}\) closed of limits \({\Rightarrow}\) if \({\mathcal{C}}\) is complete and cocomplete, so is the reflective subcategory, and limits will not change
  • coreflective subcategory a full subcategory such that \(i\) have a right adjoint
    • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) unit is natrual isomorphism fix me
  • localization of \({\mathcal{C}}\) by \(W\). \(Q: {\mathcal{C}}\to{\mathcal{C}}[W^{-1}]\) such that all \(Q(w)\) is isomorphism and \(Q\) is initial (or looser, only require up to natural isomorphism) and \(\_ \circ Q: {\mathcal{D}}^{{\mathcal{C}}[W^{-1}]}\to {\mathcal{D}}^{\mathcal{C}}\) is full and faithful
    • reflective localization when \(Q\) has a full and faithful right adjoint so it is reflective subcategory
  • completion general idea. and relation with idempotent functor

colimit

  • colimit for functor \(J:{\mathcal{D}}\to{\mathcal{C}}\) universal arrow from \(J\) to diagonal functor \(\Delta:{\mathcal{C}}\to{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathcal{D}}\). mapping out. the universal arrow is actually a set of arrows, see the picture, so it is left adjoint of \(\Delta\). we use universal cone to refer to the arrows that commute but not universal. \({\text{colim }}: {\mathcal{C}}^{\mathcal{D}}\to {\mathcal{C}}\)
    • coproduct. we use coproduct to refer coproduct of any cardinality, and finite coproduct to refer coproduct of finite cardinal
      • initial 0-ary coproduct
        • weakly initial + equalizer \({\Rightarrow}\) initial fix me
      • \(h\circ [f_1, f_2] = [h\circ f_1, h\circ f_2]\)
      • folding map \(({\text{id}}_X, {\text{id}}_X)\)
      • \(A+ B\cong B + A\). \((A + B) + C\cong A + ( B + C) \). \(C + 0 \cong C\) \({\diamond}\) using universal properties
      • of same cardinal, pushout + initial \({\Rightarrow}\) coproduct
    • coequalizer \(=<|\) of any cardinal
      • regular epimorphism is coequalizer of a pair of morphism \({\diamond}\) is epimorphism, by dueal regular monomorphism
    • pushout and wide pushout. we use pushout generally means 2-wide pushout
      • for same cardinal, coproduct + coequalizer \({\Rightarrow}\) pushout
      • if \(f\) is isomorphism so is \(q\) \({\diamond}\) trivial
      • stable under cobase change
      • understand as cluing under the two function
      • for \(f: A\to B\) define a functor \(A\downarrow {\mathcal{C}}\to B\downarrow{\mathcal{C}}\) \({\diamond}\) trivial
    • cokernel of \(f: A\to B\) pushout of \(0\leftarrow A\to B\)
      • if having zero object \({\Leftrightarrow}\) coequalizer of zero arrow
    • filtered colimit colimit from filtered category
      • directed colimit \(\underrightarrow{\lim F}\) is a colimit of upward directed set. sometimes they are called direct limit, wtf…
        • sequential colimit from ordinal, sometimes we specially use \(\omega\)
    • cocomplete
      • have all (small) colimits
      • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) coproduct and binary coequalizer \({\diamond}\) dual
    • for \(J: {\mathcal{D}}_1\times {\mathcal{D}}_2\to {\mathcal{C}}\) and natural (?), we have \({\text{colim }}_{{\mathcal{D}}_1}{\text{colim }}_{{\mathcal{D}}_2}J \cong {\text{colim }}J \cong {\text{colim }}_{{\mathcal{D}}_2}{\text{colim }}_{{\mathcal{D}}_1}J\), this is a restatement of that in functor category, limit is pointwise
      • the two push out is the same \({\diamond}\) same colimit of a diagram

more about lims

  • zero object both initial and terminal

    • zero arrow

  • lims are functorial, the one kind is trivial, sometimes they get mixed, some times we not only induce universal cone, but also universal arrow, it seems that they are all functorial

  • functor and lims, for a diagram \(J: I\to {\mathcal{C}}\) and a functor \(F:{\mathcal{C}}\to {\mathcal{D}}\), or for all limit of same type \(J\), we use lim to refer limit/colimit
    • preserve colimits. preserve limits universal cone domain \({\Rightarrow}\) image
      • cocontinuous preserve all colimits
      • continuous functor preserve all limits
    • lift limits. lift colimits when \(F\circ J\) has lim, we have a correspond lim and universal cone in domain maps to it
      • lift limits uniquely. lift colimits uniquely if the lifted ones is unique
    • reflect limits. reflect colimits for a cone in domain, universal cone image \({\Rightarrow}\) domain
    • create limits. create colimits \(J\) has lim whenever \(F\circ J\) has lim and preserve and reflect lim \({\Leftrightarrow}\) a diagram has limit when image has and a cone is universal iff the image is universal

monoidal category

  • monoidal category \({\mathcal{M}}\). unit object \(1\in {\mathcal{M}}\). functor tensor product \(\otimes:{\mathcal{M}}\times {\mathcal{M}}\to {\mathcal{M}}\). natural isomorphism associator \(a: (x\otimes y)\otimes z\leftrightarrow x\otimes (y\otimes z)\), left unitor \(l: 1\otimes x\leftrightarrow x\) and right unitor \(r:x\otimes 1\leftrightarrow x\); strict monoidal category the natural isomorphisms are all identity natural transforms

    • left closed monoidal \(\_\otimes b\), right closed monoidal \(b\otimes \_\), biclosed monoidal have right adjoint

    • \({\Leftarrow}\) braided monoidal category. natural isomorphism braiding \(B:x\otimes y\leftrightarrow y\otimes x\)

    • \({\Leftarrow}\) symmetric monoidal category \(B_{x, y}B_{y,x} = {\text{Id}}_{x\otimes y}\)

      • closed monoidal there is \([X,\_]\) right adjoint of \(\_\otimes X\), \({\text{hom}}(a\otimes b, c)\cong {\text{hom}}(a, [b, c])\). if it is left closed monoidal or right closed monodial it is biclosed monodial we preserve this to braided and symmetric

        • internal hom. internal evaluation the universal arrow. internal composition the adjoint of two evaluation \([Y, Z]\otimes [X, Y]\otimes X\to Z\) \({\diamond}\) you should always be aware how this kind of things is done, it is by \({\text{id}}\otimes f\), replacing one argument in it
          • dual object
        • in ccc notation. \((X^Y)^Z = (X^Z)^Y = X^{Y\times Z}\), \((X\times Y)^Z = X^Z\times Y^Z\)
          \({\diamond}\) Yoneda principle \({\text{hom}}(X, (A^B)^C) = {\text{hom}}(X\times C, A^B) = {\text{hom}}(X\times C\times B, A)\cong {\text{hom}}(X, A^{A\times C})\), the rest is same
        • in ccc notation. \(Y^{{\text{colim }}F}\cong \lim Y^{F_i}\). \(C^{X+Y}\cong C^X\times C^Y\) \({\diamond}\) \({\text{hom}}(X, \lim Y^{F_i})\cong \lim {\text{hom}}(X, Y^{F_i})\cong \lim{\text{hom}}(X\times F_i, Y)\) \(\cong {\text{hom}}({\text{colim }}X\times F_i, Y)\cong {\text{hom}}(X\times {\text{colim }}F_i, Y)\cong {\text{hom}}(X, Y^{{\text{colim }}F})\)
        • \(X^1 = X\) \({\diamond}\) trivial
    • \({\Leftarrow}\) cartesian monoidal category if the tensor product is product. internal hom called exponential
      • \({\Rightarrow}\) unit object is terminal object \({\diamond}\) by Yoneda, we can seee \({\text{hom}}(B, 1)\) must has exactly one element
      • cartesian closed category the \(\otimes =\times\)
        • \(1^X = 1\) \({\diamond}\) trivial
        • locally cartesian closed category

    • monoidal functor \(F: {\mathcal{C}}\to {\mathcal{C}}'\). natural transform \(\Phi: F(x)\otimes F(y)\leftrightarrow F(x\otimes y)\). morphism \(\phi: 1_{{\mathcal{C}}'}\leftrightarrow F(1_{\mathcal{C}})\). strong monoidal functor replace the morphisms to isomorphisms. strict monoidal functor replace by identity

      • braid monoidal functor. symmetric monoidal functor

    • monoidal natural transform. braided natural transform. symmetric natural transform. monoidal equivalence. braided equivalence. symmetric equivalence

model category

  • lift for commutative square , \(h: B\to X\) commutes
    • \(i\) has left lifting property and \(p\) have right lifting property, it lift exists i any lift diagram of the form
  • model category \({\mathcal{M}}\)
    • idea. see here, and here
    1. complete and cocomplete
    2. subcategory weak equivalence \({\xrightarrow{\sim}}\) closed by retraction
      1. \(f\), \(g\), \(g\circ f\) 2 of 3 \({\Rightarrow}\) 3 weak equivalence
    3. subcategory fibration \({\twoheadrightarrow}\) closed by retraction
      • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) rlp with acyclic cofibration \({\diamond}\) dual
      • stable under base change
    • acyclic fibration is both fibration and weq
      • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) rlp with cofibration
      • stable under base change
    1. subcategory cofibration \({\hookrightarrow}\) closed by retraction
      • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) having llp with respect to acyclic fibration \({\diamond}\) by 5, factoring, then lift, then retract
      • stable under cobase change \({\diamond}\) we construct the diagram, and using above, we construct another one with acyclic fibration, we induce from old one, by the pushout
    • acyclic cofibration is both cofibration and weq
      • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) llp with fibration
      • stable under cobase change
    1. given a lift diagram, a lift exists when
      1. \(i\) is cofibration and \(p\) is acyclic fibration
      2. \(i\) is acyclic cofibration and \(p\) is fibration
    2. two functorial weak factorization system, which means \((\alpha, \beta)\) is functor \({\mathcal{M}}\downarrow{\mathcal{M}}\to {\mathcal{M}}\downarrow{\mathcal{M}}\)
      1. \(f = pi\) where \(i\) is cofibration, and \(p\) is acyclic fibration
      2. \(f = pi\) where \(i\) is acyclic cofibration and \(p\) is fibration
      • strong factorization system the lift is unique
    • dual category is also model category \({\diamond}\) trivial, the axioms is self-dual
    • product category is also a model category \({\diamond}\) trivial
    • pointed category is also model category \({\diamond}\) trivial

    • cofibrant \(\emptyset\to A\) is cofibration. cofibrant replacement
      fibrant \(A\to *\) is fibration. fibrant replacement
      \({\mathcal{M}}_c\), \({\mathcal{M}}_f\), \({\mathcal{M}}_{cf}\) is subcategorys

      • Ken Brown’s lemma if functor \(F:{\mathcal{M}}\to {\mathcal{N}}\) where \({\mathcal{N}}\) has a weq subcategory and takes acyclic cofibrations between cofibrant to weq, then takes weq between them to weq \({\diamond}\)

        by pushout \(i_1\) and \(i_2\) is cofibration, so \(X\amalg Y\) and \(Z\) is cofibrant. in \({\mathcal{N}}\), \(p\) is weq, then \(f\)
    • cylinder object \(w\circ i: A\land I\) with \(A\amalg A\to A\land I{\xrightarrow{\sim}}A\) factoring the folding map
      • \(i_n = i\circ {\text{in}}_n: A\to A\land I\) for \(n = 0,1\) is weq \({\diamond}\) because \({\text{id}}_A\) is factored as \(w\circ i_n\)
      • good cylinder object \(i\) is cofibration
        • \(A\) is cofibrant \({\Rightarrow}\) \(i_n\) are acyclic cofibrations \({\diamond}\) by pushout \({\text{in}}\) is cofibration, and compose
        • very good cylinder object \(w\) is acyclic fibration \({\diamond}\) by axiom 5, at least one very good cylinder object exists
    • left homotopy \(f{\sim_l}g\), \(f,g: A\to X\). exists a (good / very good) cylinder object such that \(f + g\) extends to a map \(H: A\land I\to X\), i.e. \(H\circ (i_0+i_1 = i) = f + g\)
      • if one is weq, then another \({\diamond}\) \(f = Hi_0{\Rightarrow}H{\Rightarrow}g\)
      • \(f{\sim_l}g:A\to X\) then there is a good left homotopy. if \(X\) fibrant then there is very good left homotopy \({\diamond}\) the first follows from axiom 5. the second by factoring \(w\) as a acyclic cofibration and acyclic fibration \(A\land I\to A\land I'{\xrightarrow{\sim}}A\), then get a lift \(H': A\land I'\to X\)

      \(\pi^l(A, X)\) is equivalence class generated by left homotopy

      • if \(A\) cofibrant
        • \({\sim_l}\) is a equivalence relation on \({\text{hom}}(A, X)\) \({\diamond}\) notice that \(A\) is a pushout of ti
        • \(p: Y\to X\), is acyclic fibration, then we have bijection \(p_*: \pi^l(A, Y)\cong \pi^l(A, X)\) with \([f]\to [pf]\) \({\diamond}\) well defined because \(pH\) is the homotopy, surjective by lifting, injective again by lifting, choosing a good left homotopy
      • \(X\) is fibrant
        • \(f{\sim_l}g: A\to X\) and \(h: A'\to A\) then \(fh{\sim_l}gh\) fix me
        • we have \(\pi^l(A', A)\times \pi^l(A, X)\to \pi^l(A', X)\) by \(([h], [f])\to [fh]\) fix me
    • path object \(p\circ w: X{\xrightarrow{\sim}}X^I\to X\times X\) factoring \({\text{id}}_X\times {\text{id}}_X\)
      • \(p_n = {\text{pr}}_n\circ p\) where \(n = 0, 1\) is weq
      • good path object \(p\) is fibration
        • \(X\) is fibrant then \(p_n\) is acyclic fibrations
        • very good path object \(w\) is acyclic cofibration
    • right homotopy \(f{\sim_r}g: A\to X\) there is \(X^I\) such that \(f\times g\) lifts to \(H:A\to X^I\)
      • if one is weq, then another
      • there is good right homotopy. if \(A\) cofibrant there is very good right homotopy \({\diamond}\) dual

      \(\pi^r(A, X)\) generated

      • if \(X\) fibrant
        • it is equivalence relation
        • for \(i:A\to B\) acyclic cofibration, there is \(i^*:\pi^r(B, X)\to \pi^r(A, X)\)
      • if \(A\) is cofibrant
        • \(f{\sim_r}g\) then \(hf{\sim_r}hg {\sim_r}a\)
        • composition induce \(\pi^r(A, X)\times \pi^r(X, Y)\to \pi^r(A, Y)\)
    • \(f, g:A\to X\) fix me
      • if \(A\) cofibrant and \(f{\sim_l}g\) then \(f{\sim_r}g\)
      • if \(X\) fibrant and \(f{\sim_r}g\) then \(f{\sim_l}g\)
    • lr homotopy when \(X\to Y\) from cofibrant to fibrant \(\pi(A, X)\)
    • \(p:Z\to Y\) between fibrants, then weq \({\Leftrightarrow}\) \(p_*\) bijection
      \(i: W\to X\) between cofibrants, then weq \({\Leftrightarrow}\) \(i^*\) bijection fix me
      • when \(X\to Y\) bifibrant, \(f: A\to X\) is weq \({\Leftrightarrow}\) lr homotopy equivalence fix me
    • homotopy category \({\text{Ho}}{\mathcal{M}}\) fix me fix me: initial in the category. localization of \({\mathcal{M}}\) at \(\mathcal{W}\)
    • \(U:{\mathcal{N}}\to {\mathcal{M}}\) where \({\mathcal{N}}\) is model category
      • creates model structure weq and fibrations created by \(U\)

      • strongly creates model structure all created by \(U\)

      • with \({\mathcal{N}}\) is \(B\downarrow {\mathcal{M}}\), \({\mathcal{M}}\downarrow B\) and \((A/{\mathcal{M}}/B)_f\) and \(U\) the forgetful functor fix me
        • \(U\) strongly creates model structure \({\diamond}\) subcategory, having lims, 2, 3, 4, 5 is trivial
        • if \({\mathcal{M}}\) left/right proper model category then \({\mathcal{N}}\)
        • if \({\mathcal{M}}\) compactly/cofibrantly generated, then so \({\mathcal{N}}\)
        • the adjoints are Quillen adjoints
    • proper model category is both left proper model category and right proper model category
      • left proper model category the pushout of weq along cofibration is weq
        • \({\Leftarrow}\) every object is cofibrant fix me
        • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) left gluing lemma fix me
      • right proper model category the pullback of weq along fibration is weq
        • \({\Leftarrow}\) every object is fibrant
        • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) right gluing lemma

set

  • \({\mathcal{Set}}\)
    • object sets in \({\text{Set}}_{\mathcal{U}}\) \({\Leftrightarrow}\) 0-category
    • ZFC set theory. alternative
      • extensionality axiom
      • pairing axiom
      • union axiom
      • power axiom
        • function is defined in ZFC using this
      • replacement axiom
      • infinity axiom
      • regularity axiom has \(\in\)-minimum element
      • axiom of choice
    • morphism function
      • isomorphism bijection
      • monomorphism injection
      • epimorphism surjection
    • elementary topos
      • initial empty set. empty arrow
        • constant function requires non-empty
      • terminal unit set \(\mathbb{1}\)
      • product cartesian product
      • coproduct disjoint union
      • subobject classifer two elements set with characteristic function
      • subobject subset
        • image = coimage. canonical decomposition for sets \(A\to (A/\sim)\to {\text{im }}f\to B\)
        • preimage
        • cover
      • quotient object quotient set
      • pushout of subobjects union
      • pullback of subobjects intersection
      • equalizer by subseting \(f(x)=g(x)\)
      • coequalizer by quotienting \(f(x) = g(x)\)
      • exponential function set
      • alternating limits
        • \(\lim\inf A_i = \bigcup_i \bigcap_{j >=i} A_j\), in all but finite
        • \(\lim\sup A_i = \bigcap_i \bigcup_{j >=i} A_j\), in infinite sets
        • \(\lim A_i\)
    • sekelton categogy cardinal numbers
  • \({\mathcal{Set}_*}\) pointed category of \({\mathcal{Set}}\)
    • object pointed object in \({\mathcal{Set}}\). pointed set with basepoint

ordering

  • \({\mathcal{Pos}}\) subcategory of \({\mathcal{Set}}\)
    • object partially ordered set
      1. \(p \nless p\)
      2. \(p < q \land q < r {\Rightarrow}p < r\)
      3. \(p < q \land q < p{\Rightarrow}p = q\)
      • a preorder do not require 3, but we can always take quotient to get a pos
    • morphism order-preserving function
      • isomorphism
      • automorphism
      • subobject and monomorphism subpos
    • \((X, <)\) a pos
      • maximal \(a\in X\land \forall x :a \nless x\)
      • minimal \(a\in X\land\forall x: x \nless a\)
      • greatest element \(a\in X\land\forall x: x\leq a\), is maximal
      • least element \(a\in X \land \forall x: a\leq x\), is minimal
    • \((P, <)\) is a pos, \(X\) a subpos
      • upper bound \(\forall x: x \leq a\)
      • lower bound \(\forall x: a \leq x\)
      • sup least element of upper bounds
      • inf greatest element of lower bounds
    • product product order \({\diamond}\) trivial
    • Zorn’s lemma every linear subset has upper bound then has maximal element

  • order-complete a pos such that every non-empty subset having upper bound has sup \({\Leftrightarrow}\) having lower bound has inf \({\diamond}\) trivial

  • directed set a preorder having finite upper bound
    • product product directed set with product order \({\diamond}\) trivial
  • lattice a partially ordered set such that any two element has sup and inf
    • \({\S}\) \({\mathbb{Z}}\) by divisibility
    • complete lattice all subset has sup and inf
  • \({\mathcal{Los}}\) full subcategory of \({\mathcal{Pos}}\)
    • object linear ordering
      1. \(\forall p,q: p < q \lor p = q \lor q < p\)
    • morphism
      • increasing function which is just order-preserving function
      • decreasing function
      • strict increasing function
      • strict decreasing function
  • subcategory of \({\mathcal{Los}}\)
    • object well ordering linear ordering that every non-empty subset has least
      • initial segment
    • morphism
      • increasing function of well-ordering \(f(x) \ge x\)
      • isomorphism \(W_1\) and \(W_2\) only has unique isomorphism
        • no well ordered set is isomorphic to it’s initial segment
      • automorphism the only automorphism is \({\text{id}}\)
      • for \(W_1\) and \(W_2\) or they are isomorphic, or one is isomorphic to other’s initial segment
    • by axiom of choose every set can be well-ordered
    • skeleton category ordinal numbers

ordinal

  • \(\mathcal{Ord}\) skeleton category of well ordered set
    • object ordinal number transitive set and well ordered by \(\in\)
      • transitive set \(T\) such that \(x\in T{\Rightarrow}x\subset T\)
      1. \(\emptyset\) is ordinal
      2. if \(\alpha\) is ordinal, \(\beta\in \alpha\) then \(\beta\) is ordinal. proof by chaining…
      3. \(\alpha \subset \beta{\Rightarrow}\alpha\in\beta\)
      4. either \(\alpha \subset \beta\) or \(\beta\subset\alpha\)
      5. define the order by \(<\) is \(\in\), so \(\mathcal{Ord}\) is linear ordered and is well ordered
      • successor ordinal and limit ordinal

cardinal

  • skeleton category of \({\mathcal{Set}}\)
    • object cardinal an ordinal such that \(\forall b < a: |a| \neq |b|\)
      • what it means by: \(|X| = |Y|\) and \(|X| < |Y|\)
      • \(|X| < |P(X)|\)
      • Cantor-Bernstein theorem \(|A| \leq |B| \land |B| \leq |A| {\Rightarrow}|A| = |B|\)
    • cardinal arithmetic
      • \(a + b = |A\cup B|\) disjoint
      • \(a \cdot b = |A\times B|\)
      • \(a^b = |A^B|\)
        • \(|P(A)| = 2^{|A|}\)
    • finite cardinal
      • finite and infinite
    • aleph is limit ordinal
      • \(\aleph_0 = \omega_0 = \omega\)
      • \(\aleph_\alpha \cdot \aleph_\alpha= \aleph_\alpha\)
      • \(\aleph_\alpha + \aleph_\beta= \aleph_\alpha \cdot \aleph_\beta = \max(\aleph_\alpha, \aleph_\beta)\)
    • \(|\mathbb{R}| = 2^{\aleph_0}\)
      • continuum hypothesis \(2^{\aleph_0} = \aleph_1\)

monoid

  • pointed category \({\mathcal{Mon}}\)
    • object monoid \((S\in{\mathcal{Set}}, *:S\times S\to S, e\in S)\)
      1. unit element \(\forall a\in S: ae = ea\)
        • unique. \(e = ee' = e'\)
      2. associative law of composition \(abc = a(bc)\)
        • product of zero element well defined \({\diamond}\) trivial
        • product of infinite but almost all zero set, well defined \({\diamond}\) trivial
        • \(\forall m,n\in {\mathbb{N}}: a^n a^m = a^{m + n}\). this is not free! proofed using associative
        • the above can be generalized to endomorphism
      • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) a monoid object in \({\mathcal{Set}}\)
      • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) a category with one object
    • morphism homomorphism \(f(e) = e\), \(f(ab) = f(a) f(b)\) \({\Leftrightarrow}\) go homomorphism in \({\mathcal{Set}}\) \({\Leftrightarrow}\) functor between one object category
    • subobject submonoid

  • \({\mathcal{GP}}\) full subcategory of \({\mathcal{Cat}}\)
    • object groupoid a category such that every morphism is isomorphism
    • connected groupoid as a category is connected category
      • \({\Rightarrow}\) skeleton category is one element with full isomorphisms, so a group \({\diamond}\) trivial

group

idea

  • group is about symmetric
    • for example the idea of normal subgroup, it is about \(hghg = hhgg\) kind of thing
    • see this and this for how to think about groups

group

  • \({\mathcal{Grp}}\) reflective subcategory and coreflective subcategory of \({\mathcal{Mon}}\) \({\diamond}\) here
    • object group
      1. monoid
      2. inverse \(xy = yx = e\) is unique, as proofed in any category
        • group multiplication always give an isomorphism as set, multiple by inverse is the inverse map
      • \(n,m \in \mathbb{Z}\), \(a^n a^m = a^{n+m}\)
        • power arithmetic is well defined
      • actually left unit and left inverse is sufficient
      • trivial group is smallest group
      • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) a group object in \({\mathcal{Set}}\) \({\Leftrightarrow}\) a groupoid with single object
    • morphism homomorphism only \(f(ab) = f(a)f(b)\) is sufficient, because \(f(e)= f(e)f(e){\Rightarrow}e = f(e)\)
      • kernel \({\text{ker }}f\) is a normal subgroup \({\Leftrightarrow}\) quotient group \(gN = Ng\) \({\diamond}\) trivial
        • how to consider this? if you consider what things can be a morphism in group, then you will notice that only when we make the group structure fuzz… kind of. then you want to make certain subgroup into a point, because subgroups has the good property of having boundary of coset. but \(aHbH = abH\) will require that \(aH = Ha\) for it
        • consider as maps, you have a subset of maps, that if you perform out of order, then you do not go out of the subset
        • universal properties, notice that cokernel only exists in abeblian group and modules
        • closed under intersection \({\diamond}\) kernel for product
        • closed by inverse map \({\diamond}\) kernel for chaining \(G\to F\to F/k\). but not for morphism
        • closed by \(N_1N_2\) fix me: can we found a map like above?
        • normalizer \(N_H\) is a group
          • if \(K\subset N_H\) then \(KH\) is a subgroup
        • Weyl group \(W_H = N_H / H\)
        • Dedekind group every subgroup is normal
        • Hamiltonian group Dedekind group but not abelian group
          • \({\S}\) quaternion group
        • simple group nor-trivial and no non-trivial normal subgroup
      • image \({\text{im }}f\) is a subgroup \({\diamond}\)
        1. because we know that it contains the unit
        2. and just from \(f(ab) = f(a)f(b)\) we know that it is closed by multiplication and inverse
      • group morphism structure is determined by values on generator. there is two kind of things: group morphism is not defined by values on generator, because it might not be well defined, but it is determined by values on generator, when using this word, we know prior it is a group morphism
      • monomorphism
        • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) kernel is trivial
        • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) injective
          \({\diamond}\) injective \({\Rightarrow}\) kernel and monomorphism is trivial, kernel to injective by unique inverse, monomorphism to kernel is by considering two map \({\mathbb{Z}}\to C_a\)
      • epimorphism
        • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) surjective
          \({\diamond}\) a proof in ref-category-for. suppose \(f: G\to H\) is epimorphism but not surjective, let \(M = {\text{im }}f\), if \((H:M) = 2\) then we know that \(M\) is normal, so…. if \((H:M)>2\), we have three different cosets of \(M\), \(M\), \(Mu\) and \(Mv\), we define the magical permutation. then we ensure that it maps same on \(M\), but not the same on one coset
      • isomorphism
        • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) bijective
        • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) monomorphism + epimorphism
          \({\diamond}\) isomorphism is monomorphism and epimorphism so it is bijective. if it is bijective, we can define the inverse map by just function inverse
    • group generator \(G = \langle S \rangle\) every element finitely expressed \({\Leftrightarrow}\) \(F(S)\to G\) is epimorphism. finitely generated group
    • subobject subgroup a subset such that inclusion is homomorphism
      • contains \(e\) and closed by loc and inverse
      • coset. left coset. right coset of subgroup \({\diamond}\)
        1. proof that it is equivalence relation, because subgroup closed, and we have inverse
        2. cosets have same cardinality, no matter left or right. this is again by closed also having inverse because \(H^{-1} = H{\Rightarrow}aH \leftrightarrow Ha^{-1}\)
      • \((G:H)\) index of subgroup the cardinal of cosets
        • group index theorem \((G:H)(H:K) = (G:K)\). we are actually doing cardinal arithmetic \({\diamond}\) by giving the bijection, doing this is actually highly trivial, but the result is not trivial at all, we mostly used the covering
        • order of group \((G:1)\)
      • index \(2\) subgroup is normal \({\diamond}\) cosets is disjoint, and \(H = H\)
  • canonical decomposition
    \({\diamond}\) we define the map by \(aH\to f(a)\) because for all \(y\in H\), \(f(xy) = f(x)\) we see it is a well defined map, then we see that it is a morphism, and it is unique
    • canonical isomorphisms
      • canonical group isomorphism 0 \(\frac{G_1\times G_2}{H_1\times H_2}\cong\frac{G_1}{H_1}\times\frac{G_2}{H_2}\)
        \({\diamond}\) first we proof that \(H_1\times H_2\) is normal, this is trivial, then we show that if \(g_i H_1\) and \(g_j H_2\) then \((g_i, g_j) H_1\times H_2\), we define the map and show it is a morphism, then we show the kernel is trivial and it is surjective, so it is an isomorphism
      • canonical group isomorphism 1 \(\frac{G/H}{N/H}\cong\frac{G}{N}\), \(H\subset N\) normal
        \({\diamond}\) first all \(A/B\) is well defined, then we see that left is well defined. for \(G/H\) and \(G/N\) we define a map \(gH\to gN\), we see that it is a morphism, then we show that the kernel is \(N/H\)
      • canonical group isomorphism 2 \(\frac{H}{H\cap K}\cong \frac{HK}{K}\), \(H\subset N_K\), so \(HK = KH\) is subgroup, and \(H\cap K\) is normal in \(H\)
        \({\diamond}\) proof that \(H\cap K\) is normal in \(H\), \(hk = k_2 h\) and \(k \in H\) so \(k_2 \in H\) so normal. we define the map \(h\to hK\), then we show that it is morphism and it’s kernel is \(H\cap K\)
        • this means you have normalizer, you can add/subtract the irrelevant dims to the problem. notice that \(K\) is not always subgroup of \(H\)
      • butterfly lemma
        1. \(U\), \(V\) subgroup of \(G\)
        2. \(u\), \(v\) normal subgroup of \(U\), \(V\)
        3. then \(\frac{u(U\cap V)}{u(U\cap v)}\cong\frac{(U\cap V)v}{(u\cap V)v}\cong\frac{U\cap V}{(u\cap V)(U\cap v)}\)
        • \({\diamond}\) proof of the group is normal. if \(a\in U\cap V\) we have \(a(u\cap V) = (u\cap V)b\) where \(b\) exists and is in \(U\) because \(u\) normal in \(U\), and it is in \(V\) because \(V\) is closed. so it is normal. then we use canonical group isomorphism 2, we should show that \(((u\cap V)v)\cap(U\cap V) = (u\cap V)(U\cap v)\), which should be trivial. it is tedious and not instructive, but that’s kind of the fact of my mathematical ability now

      • the automorphisms is fully described by map on generator \({\diamond}\) trivial
      • if \(p\) is prime, then \((({\mathbb{Z}}/p^r{\mathbb{Z}})^*, \cdot)\) is cyclic, except for \(p = 2 \land r \geq 3\) which is of \((2, 2^{r-2})\) \({\diamond}\)
        1. \(r = 1\) case. it is an abelian group, so there should be one maximal order element \(g\), so \(h^{|g|} = 1\) for all \(h\), but we have \(x^d = 1\) at most have \(d\) solutions in \({\mathbb{Z}}/p{\mathbb{Z}}\), so \(|g|\geq p - 1\)
        2. for general \(p \neq 2\), we expand \((p + 1)^{up^m} -1 \) and inspect the pattern of order of \(p\) in each term. in first \(p^2 -1\) term, there is no decreasing, only stay in one place at \(p\) term, with order \(p^{m + p -1}\), and at \(p^k\) it decrease \(k -1\) with order \(p^{m + p^k - k}\), clearly all order bigger than \(p^{m + 1}\)
        3. so \((p +1)\) is of order \(p^{r -1}\)
        4. by \(\text{gcd}(p^{r-1}, p-1) = 1\), we get the group is cyclic of order \(p^{r-1}(p -1)\)
        5. for \(p = 2\) we have group order \(2^{r-1}\), it is almost the same expanding, but we show that it is of same divider by sum of 2nd and 4th term

action

  • category \({\mathcal{Act}}(G)\)
    • object the action functor with the target set. \(G\)-set
      • a left action of group \(G\) on \(A\) in category \({\mathcal{C}}\), is a morphism \(\sigma:G \to {\text{aut}}_{\mathcal{C}}(A)\), or viewed as a functor from single element category to category of \(A\). we mostly works in category \({\mathcal{Set}}\), with a object \(S\)
    • isotropy of point \(s\), \(G_s\). \(\{g|gs = s\}\)
      • isotropy groups is conjugate if \(\exists gs = s'\)
      • faithful action’s isotropy is always \(\{e\}\)
      • fixed point of group action \(s\) such that \(G_s = G\)
    • orbit of \(s\), \(Gs\) is a sub-\(G\)-set, and orbit
      • transitive action, \(\forall s, s', \exists g: gs = s'\) or group is single orbit
      • orbit decomposition formula \(Gs\cong G/G_s\) as \(G\)-set. \(S\cong \coprod G/G_{s_i}\), where \(s_i\) is representatives of different orbit
    • morphism \(G\)-map function \(f\) such that \(f(gx) = g(f(x))\)
      • if \(G\) act transitively on \(S\), then the \(G\)-map automorphisms \({\text{aut}}_G(S)\cong W_{G_s}\), fixing \(s\), by \(nG_s\to (gs\to gns)\) \({\diamond}\)
        1. all element is some \(g_0s\), we can pick a representatives \(g_i\)
        2. then \(g_0s\) maps to \(g_0 ns\) which is independent of representative \(n\), and is a set automorphism because if \(g_0n_0s = g_1n_0s{\Rightarrow}g^{-1}g_0\in n_0^{-1}G_s n_0 = G_s\)
        3. it is trivially a \(G\)-map, by \(n(gs) = gns\)
        4. we show that actually this is all automorphism. for automorphism \(\varphi: S\to S\), we have \(\varphi(s) = ns\) for some \(n\in G\), for \(h\in G_s\), \(hns = \varphi(hs) = \varphi(s) = ns\), so \(n^{-1}hn\in G_s\). and \(\varphi\) is determined by single element \(n\), we are done
        • a example of non-transitive \(G\)-map which is not included in the action is easily shown, consider two copy of same orbit
    • \({\S}\)
      • \(A\) conjugate to \(B\) is \(\exists x: B = xAx^{-1}\), \(G\) actions on conjugate subgroups of \(A\)
        1. transitive action, because of the definition
        2. there are a lot of subgroup isomorphisms that is not working by conjugation, consider \({\mathbb{Z}}\times {\mathbb{Z}}\)
        3. number of conjugate group of \(H\) is equal to \(G:N_H\)
        • category of \({\text{hom}}(G, H)\) with morphisms the \(hfh^{-1}\) fix me
      • class formula. \(G\) acts by conjugate on \(G\). \((G:1) = \sum |Gs| = \sum (G:G_x)\), taken from different conjugate classes. consider \(G\) action by conjugate on \(G\) viewing as an set, then… for example, when \(z\) is in center, \(G_x = G\) because all \(xzx^{-1} = z\), and it’s orbit is just \(z\)
  • \({\mathcal{O}}(G)\) subcategory of \({\mathcal{Act}}(G)\)
    • object canonical orbit \(G/H\) (so the action is all transitive action, just by the name)
    • morphism \(G\)-maps. \(\alpha: G/H\to G/K\) has form \(\alpha(gH) = g\gamma K\) where \(\gamma\in G\) satisfies \(\gamma^{-1}H\gamma \subset K\) \({\diamond}\)
      1. if \(\alpha(eH) = \gamma K\) then \(\gamma K = \alpha(eH) = \alpha(hH) = h\alpha(eH) = h\gamma K\), so \(\gamma^{-1}h\gamma\in K\) for all \(h\in H\), and we know what \(\alpha\) is determined by \(\gamma\)
      2. if we defined like above, we have if \(g_1g_2^{-1}\in H\), then \(g_1\gamma K = g_2 \gamma K{\Rightarrow}\gamma^{-1}g_2^{-1}g_1\gamma K = K{\Rightarrow}K = K\), so the set automorphism is well defined. and we trivially has \(G\)-set automorphism
      • the category \({\mathcal{O}}(G)\) is isomorphism to \(\mathcal{G}\) with objects subgroups of \(G\) and morphisms distinct subconjugacy relations \(\gamma^{-1}H\gamma\subset G\) for \(\gamma\in G\)
  • groupoid action \(T:{\mathcal{B}}\to \mathcal{Set}\). a functor that maps to isomorphisms, so for each \(b\) in \({\mathcal{B}}\), \(T\) restricts to an group action on \(T(b)\)
    • transitive groupoid action if for each \(b\), it is an transitive action
      • for groupoid connected groupoid, if this is true for one, then true for all. because groupoid is an category with only isomorphism

finite group

  • \({\mathcal{FinGrp}}\) subcategory of \({\mathcal{Grp}}\)
    • when dealing with finite group, it is just ok to think of what multi table it have, and think how many distinct elements you have
    • finite group
    • \(p\)-group finite group of order \(p^n\) where \(p\) prime
    • \(p\)-Sylow subgroup
    1. finite abelian group \(G\) has subgroup order \(p\) dividing \(|G|\) \({\diamond}\) easy if you know facts about finitely generated abelian group
    2. \(p\)-Sylow group exists
      \({\diamond}\) the proof is standard, i think i cannot come up with a proof of this myself, because i am too dumb… just repeat it myself… if \(G\) has order \(p^n\) we are done, so we assume not. we consider proper subgroup of \(G\), if there exists a subgroup \(H\) such that \(p^n\) divides \(|H|\) then we are done, because we can induction. suppose not, then we have in \(|G| = \sum (G:G_x) = |Z| + \sum (G:G_x)\) the right must all dividable by \(p\), so is \(|Z|\). and we can construct a group of order \(p\) in \(Z\). and we can factor it to induction again, and project back and show that the group has order \(p^n\)
    3. \(H\) a \(p\) group actioning on finite set \(S\), then number of fix points is \(|S|\) mod \(p\)
      \({\diamond}\) using the orbit decomposition formula, \(|S| = \sum (H: H_{s_i}) = |F| \sum (H:H_{s_1})\) the right part must divide \(p\), so \(|S|-|F|\) divides \(p\)
    4. \(p\) group is contained in some \(p\)-Sylow group
    5. all \(p\)-Sylow group is conjugate
    6. number of \(p\)-Sylow group is \(1\mod p\)
      \({\diamond}\) we follow the proof by Lang. first if \(H\) is contained in normalizer of \(P\), then \(H\subset P\), this is proofed by seeing the prime indexes, we used the condition \(p\)-Sylow as maximal \(p\). the next part is by taking \(S\) all conjugate class of \(P\) in \(G\), then we have \(|S|\) is not divisible by \(p\), then we have \(H\) also operate on \(S\) and it cannot have one fixed point, which means it is contained in some \(aPa^{-1}\). by taking \(H = aPa^{-1}\) we show that it is in \(S\). and \(H\) has only one fixed point, showing that mod 1.
      \({\diamond}\) the proof is essentially number theoretic, so the particular group structure is caused by finiteness and number theory
    7. \(p\)-group has non-trivial center, thus solvable, and also has cyclic tower
      \({\diamond}\) it is again a trivial application of class formula
    • smallest prime index group is normal
      \({\diamond}\) if \(N_H=H\) we have \(G\) operates on \(G/H\) and so we have a subgroup into a group of order \(p!\) and we show that the kernel must be \(H\), i.e. \(hgHg^{-1}h^{-1} = gHg^{-1}{\Rightarrow}g^{-1}hg \in H\)
    • order \(pq\), \(p\neq q\) and primes, then solvable \({\diamond}\) trivial by above lemma

abelian group

  • \({\mathcal{Ab}}\)
    • object abelian group
      • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) group object in \({\mathcal{Grp}}\)
    • morphism
      • for natural isomorphism \({\text{Id}}\cong {\text{Id}}\). \(n\) power map exists, because commutative, the deeper result is that abelian group is essentially an module over \({\mathbb{Z}}\)
      • homomorphism in \({\mathcal{Ab}}\) is again a abelian group
    • product finite case is direct sum
    • coproduct direct sum \(G\oplus H\), only finite non zero
      • proof that is is coproduct. we should be using a infinite sum now, and etc.
    • pullback is the subgroup of \(A\times B\) such that \(f(a) = g(b)\)
    • free object free abelian group \({\mathcal{Set}}\). \(F^{ab}(A)\). \(\bigoplus_{i\in A} {\mathbb{Z}}_i\)
      • base non-empty and \(A\) is unique expressed \(x =\sum x_i e_i\) for \(x_i\in {\mathbb{Z}}\) almost all zero
      • \(f:A\to B\) surjective and \(B\) is free, then we can find \(C\subset A\) free such that \(f(C)\cong B\) and \(A = {\text{ker }}f \oplus C\) \({\diamond}\) using base
      • subgroup is also free abelian group, and has smaller cardinal base
        • special case of free module over principal entire ring fix me

      1. we proof by showing the map in surjective, injective by showing the kernel is trivial. surjective using the relative prime decomposition
      2. abelian \(p\)-group isomorphism to product of cyclic \(p\)-groups
        • fix me

        • proof by hand. i do not want to proof again, this is basically the relative prime thing

        • can we proof it using the structure of torsion group? i think a problem is that there might be no maximal order at all. we proof that the cyclic group all have order smaller than one number, if not, we have no maximal order element. so we can pick the maximal order of each one, and proofed
    • finitely generated abelian group \(A = A_{\text{tor}}\oplus B\) where \(B\) is free \({\diamond}\)

ring

  • \({\mathcal{Ring}}\)
    • object ring
      1. abelian group on \(+\)
      2. monoid \(\cdot\)
      3. distributive \((r+s)t = rt + st\)
      • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) ring object in \({\mathcal{Set}}\)
      • \(0r = r0 = 0\)
      • there are many things without \(1\), for example \(2{\mathbb{Z}}\)
      • left unit of ring has right inverse, right unit of ring has left inverse, unit of ring \(A^*\), invertible elements of \(A\)
        • \(u\) left unit of ring \({\Leftrightarrow}\) left multiplication by \(u\) is surjective
        • \(u\) left unit of ring \({\Rightarrow}\) right multiplication is injective, and so is not right zero divisor
        • left unit of ring is not always unique, but unit of ring is unique
        • shift operator bad example~!
        • units is a group
        • in \({\mathbb{Z}}\) we have \(-1\) and \(1\) is unit!!! not only \(1\)!!!
      • left zero divisor \(a\) such \(\exists b: ab = 0\), right zero divisor
        • nilpotent \(a^n = 0\) is zero divisor \(1+ n\) is unit
        • \([2][3] = [0]\)
        • not left zero divisor \({\Leftrightarrow}\) left multiplication is injective
    • morphism \(f(a + b) = f(a) + f(b)\), \(f(ab) = f(a)f(b)\), \(f(1) = 1\), or say that it is a map such that is the corresponding group morphism and monoid morphism
      • image \({\text{im }}f\) is subring
      • monomorphism \({\Leftrightarrow}\) \({\text{ker }}f = \{0\}\) \({\Leftrightarrow}\) injective
      • epimorphism \({\Leftarrow}\) surjective
      • isomorphism \({\Leftarrow}\) bijective
        • monomorphism and epimorphism is not necessary isomorphism
      • \(\lambda: R\to {\text{end}}_{{\mathcal{Ab}}}(R)\), is injective ring homomorphism
    • subobject subring additive subgroup, contains \(1\), closed by \(\cdot\)
      • center of ring \(Z_R\), the center viewed a multiplicative group. \(\{0, 1\}\subset Z_R\)
    • initial \({\mathbb{Z}}\)
      • characteristic of ring
      • for field \(K\), the characteristic \(0\) fields contains an image of \({\mathbb{Q}}\) and for characteristic \(p\) contains some isomorphic image of \(F_p\). smallest subfield contains \(1\) and has no trivial automorphism. and prime ring we means \({\mathbb{Z}}\) or \(F_p\) in it
    • final zero ring is smallest ring
    • ring generators of \(B\) over \(A\), \(S\). \(B = A[S]=\sum as^is^j\cdots s^k\) with \(A\) commutate with \(S\)
      • \(B\) finitely generated over \(A\)
      • in Atiyah, a finitely generated ring is when viewed as \({\mathbb{Z}}\)-algebra
      • noetherian ring every left ideal is finitely generated. or noetherian module when viewed as a left module over itself
    • product exists and is like group product. it is not pointed category
    • coproduct see Wikipedia
    • left ideal additive subgroup and \(A\alpha\subset \alpha\), right ideal \(\alpha A = \alpha\), ideal \(\alpha = A\alpha = \alpha A\). quotient ring
      • ideal is submodule of the ring
      • ideal is kernel, kernel is ideal
        \({\diamond}\) we define \((x + \alpha)(y + \alpha) = (xy + \alpha)\) we show that it is well defined, we show that it is representative independent, and it is associative hand has unit, and distributive. we used \(xa \in\alpha\) in the proof that it is well defined. just like the normal group case… this is indeed wired, the normal groups has inherited product, but ring have not, consider \((2 + {\mathbb{Z}}/12{\mathbb{Z}})(3 + {\mathbb{Z}}/12{\mathbb{Z}})\), if we define it be the inherited products, then it will not include \(6 + 12\) and will not be a coset, that’s why the product is defined differently. the unit of the cosets is \(1+\alpha\) and the zero is \(\alpha\), this is because it is an ideal and it is kind of like a zero which takes everything back
      • an ideal contains \(1\) must be \(A\)
      • for ideals, \(\alpha\beta = \{x_1y_1 + x_2y_2 + \cdots+ x_ny_n\}\) is an ideal
        • it forms a groupoid, with unit \(A\)
        • same for left ideals
      • \(\alpha + \beta\) viewed as normal additive subgroup is left, right two sided ideal

      • intersection closed for left, right, twoside
      • \(\alpha \cap (\beta_1 + \beta_2) = \alpha\cap \beta_1 + \alpha\cap \beta_2\) if \(\alpha\subset \beta_1\) or \(\alpha \subset \beta_2\)
      • left principal ideal \(Aa\), \(aA\), \(AaA\)
      • generators for ideal is a left ideal \(x_1a_1 + x_2a_2 + \cdots + x_na_n\) with \(x_i\in A\) is generated by \((a_1, a_2,..., a_n)\)
    • canonical decomposition

localization

  • localization ring of fractions of ring \(A\) by multiplicative submonoid \(S\), \(S^{-1}A\)
    1. \((a,s)\sim(a',s'){\Leftrightarrow}s_1(s'a-sa') = 0\), by \(a/s\)
    2. we define \((a/s)(a'/s') = aa'/ss'\). proof well defined
    3. \(\frac{a}{s} +\frac{a'}{s'}=\frac{s'a+sa'}{ss'}\). proof well defined
    4. it is a ring
      1. additive group
        • associative \({\diamond}\) \(\frac{as_bs_c+s_abs_c+s_as_bc}{s_as_bs_c}\)
        • unit, inverse \({\diamond}\) trivial
      2. multiplicative monoid \({\diamond}\) trivial
      3. distributive \({\diamond}\) trivial
      • \((a, 1) = 0{\Rightarrow}\exists b\in S: ab = 0\)
      • if \(0\in S\) then \(S^{-1}A\) zero ring
    • for \(f: A\to B\) we do not necessary have some \(f':S_A^{-1}A\to S_B^{-1}B\). example: \({\mathbb{Z}}\to {\mathbb{Z}}/p{\mathbb{Z}}\)
    • \(\varphi_S: A\to S^{-1}A\) such that \(a\to a/1\), and every element in \(\varphi_S(S)\) is invertible, by \(1/s\), and this is universal in such morphisms (making \(S\) invertible!!!). or say, it is initial in some category
      • it is an initial, and this means factor though, so what we should do is find the unique initial map that is induced by the actual map). proof by for such a \(f:A\to B\) we have \(f(s_1)(f(s')f(a)-f(s)f(a'))=0\) to we have \(f(a)f(s)^{-1}=f(a')f(s')^{-1}\) if we send \(a/s\) to \(f(a)f(s)^{-1}\) it is well defined and it is homomorphism and commutative, and unique
      • if additionally we have \(f(a) = 0{\Leftrightarrow}\exists s\in S: as =0\) and every element is \(f(a)f(s)^{-1}\), then we have \(h: S^{-1}A\cong B\) and \(f = h\circ \varphi_S\). proof is trivial
      • if \(A\) is entire and \(S\) do not contains \(0\) then \(\varphi_S\) is injective
    • \(S\) is non-zero elements, then we have quotient field of \(A\) and it is an field

    4. \(\_\otimes_R N\) left-adjoint to \({\text{hom}}_R(N, \_)\)
      1. functor with \(\alpha\otimes N: M_1\otimes N\to M_2\otimes N\) by induce on \(M_1\times N\to M_2\times N\to M_2\otimes N\)

      2. \({\text{hom}}_R(M\otimes_R N, M')\cong {\text{hom}}_R(M, {\text{hom}}_R(N, M'))\)
        1. for any \(f: M\otimes_R N\to M'\) we can define a linear map from \(M\) to \({\text{hom}}_R(N, M')\) simply
        2. for any \(g: M\to {\text{hom}}_R(N, M')\) we can define a map \(g':M\times N\to M'\) by \((m, n)\to g(m)(n)\). it is linear in second variable, as it is a linear map. it is linear in first variable, as \(g'((a, n_0) + (b, n_0)) = g'((a, n_0)) + g'((b, n_0)) = g(a)(n_0) + g(b)(n_0)\) as \(g(a + b) = g(a) + g(b)\), the former \(=g(a + b)(n_0) = g'((a+b, n_0))\). so we have induced map
        3. it is easily see that they both add to \({\text{id}}\)
      3. cocontinuous
        • \((M\oplus N) \otimes P \cong (M\otimes P)\oplus (N\otimes P)\)

algebra

  • \(R\)-\({\mathcal{Alg}}= R\downarrow {\mathcal{CRing}}\)
    • object algebra a ring homomorphism from a commutative ring \(f: R\to S\) such that \(f(R)\subset Z_S\) \({\Leftrightarrow}\) a ring which is a \(R\)-module, and \(r(ab) = r(a)b = ar(b)\)
    • morphism \(f: A\to B\) such that \(f(ra) = rf(a)\)
      • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) both ring and module morphism
      • \({\Leftrightarrow}\) \(h\circ f = g\)
    • finite algebra \(f: R\to S\), \(S\) finitely generated as \(R\)-module
      • please actually check it, it is like free module
      • finite type algebra there is finite \(x_i\in S\) such that every \(B\) is polynomial of \(x_i\) with coefficients in \(f(R)\) \({\Leftrightarrow}\) there is a \(A\)-algebra epimorphism from \(A[x_1, \ldots, x_n]\to B\)
        • \(R[x]\) is finite type algebra, but not finite algebra
    • tensor product of algebra \(D = B\otimes_A C\) \({\diamond}\)
      1. with \(\sum(b_i\otimes c_i)\sum(b'_j\otimes c'_j) = \sum_{i,j}b_ib_j'\otimes c_ic'_j\). it is a ring, and if \(B\) and \(C\) is commutative ring, is is too
      • it is a \(A\)-algebra, by \(a\to f(a)\otimes 1 = 1\otimes g(a)\), it is well defined, consider how \(A\) operate on \(B\) and \(C\) as a module
    • \({\S}\)
      • ring is \({\mathbb{Z}}\) algebra, by \(3ab = a(3b) = (3a)b\) which follows form distributivity of ring, and any ring morphism is an \(R\)-algebra morphism
      • group ring \(R[G]\)
  • associative algebra a ring without unit and a module
